Yet, there is nothing to suggest that these employees have adequately dealt with truancy.	None
As mentioned, this is, in part, due to the economic conditions in which many parents find themselves.	None
Financial assistance or incentives must be given.	None
Only then, may the Government actively enforce compulsory attendance legislation.	None
We must also remember that it is not only the child who fails to attend but also the child who fails to enroll that has violated an attendance law.	None
Before taking issue with State Union Territory compulsory education statutes, I note that education has traditionally been reserved for the States.	None
Only in 1976, vide the 42nd Amendment of the Constitution, did education become a part of Concurrent List of Schedule 7.	None
In its 165th Report, the Law Commission of India has also recommended enactment of Central Legislation in this respect.	None
Putting education in the Concurrent List turns out to be a positive development, given the States failure to provide effective legislation.	None
The States laws fail on two accounts.	None
First, they are too lenient to have a deterrent effect.	None
Second, the legislation is not adequately enforced, in part, because it does not require police officers to do the job.	None
If we analyze the legislation passed by different States, another conclusion becomes obvious no State has provided for an adequate punishment whose effect would be to deter citizens from committing a violation.	None
It is necessary to reproduce some of the various compulsory education laws of the States.	None
Under Section 7 of The Tamil Nadu Compulsory Elementary Education Act, 1994 Every parent or guardian of a child of school age who fails to discharge his duty under section 4 duty of parent to cause child to attend elementary school shall be punishable with fine which may extend to one hundred rupees.	None
Section 18(1) of The Delhi Primary Education Act, 1960 states	None
If any parent fails to comply with an attendance order passed under Section 13, he shall be punishable with fine not exceeding two rupees, and, in the case of continuing contravention, with an additional fine not exceeding fifty naye paise for every day during which such contravention continues after conviction for the first of such contraventions.	None
Provided that the amount of fine payable by any one person in respect of any child in any one year shall not exceed fifty rupees.	None
Analysis of these State laws reveals that they are weak in character and perhaps have never been implemented.	None
If we compare these laws with their sister statutes in United States, we realize that the U.S. laws are far stronger.	None
In Wisconsin, parents who fail to send their children to school may have to pay a fine of not more than 500 or face imprisonment for not more than 30 days or both.	None
Wisconsin Statute Sections 118.15(1)(a) and 118.15(5)(a)1.a.	None
For a second or subsequent offense, they may face a fine of not more than 1,000 or imprisonment for not more than 90 days or both.	None
Wisconsin Statute Sections 118.15(1)(a) and 118.15(5)(a)1.b.	None
Alternatively, they may be sentenced to perform community service.	None
Wisconsin Statute Sections 118.15(1)(a) and 118.15(5)(a)2.	None
Unlike Wisconsin, Tamil Nadu and Delhis laws have no teeth.	None
The other main problem is implementation of these laws.	None
Neither the State Governments nor their police agencies are at all serious about implementing these compulsory laws.	None
There are hardly any cases where even fines have been imposed.	None
Some form of compulsory education has been on the statute books since 1917.	None
We have seen Western countries enforce these laws.	None
Most Western countries enjoy almost universal literacy while 35 of our population is illiterate.	None
While a robust financial incentive programme may not have been possible in 1917, it is today.	None
If we wish to develop further, we must educate each and every citizen aged six to fourteen.	None
In order to give effect to the constitutional right under Article 21A, it is imperative that the Central Government pass suitable legislation.	None
The fine should be suitably increased.	None
Imprisonment should be a sentencing option as well.	None
The current patchwork of State UT legislation on compulsory education is insufficient.	None
Small monetary fines do not go far enough to ensure the implementation of Article 21A. A disclaimer is attached to these recommendations.	None
The recommendations for the enforcement of compulsory education are contingent upon the implementation of a financial incentive program that would make education viable for the poor.	None
The carrot must come before the stick.	None
If there is no financial incentive program in place, the Government cannot expect the poorest of the poor to send their children to school.	None
The Parliament should criminally penalize those parents who receive financial benefits and, despite such payments, send their children to work and penalize those employers who preclude children from attending school or completing homework.	None
It has become necessary that the Government set a realistic target within which it must fully implement Article 21A regarding free and compulsory education for the entire country.	None
The Government should suitably revise budget allocations for education.	None
The priorities have to be set correctly.	None
The most important fundamental right may be Article 21A, which, in the larger interest of the nation, must be fully implemented.	None
Without Article 21A, the other fundamental rights are effectively rendered meaningless.	None
Education stands above other rights, as ones ability to enforce ones fundamental rights flows from ones education.	None
This is ultimately why the judiciary must oversee Government spending on free and compulsory education.	None
At the same time, spending is an area in which the judiciary must not overstep its constitutional mandate.	None
The power of the purse is found in Part V, Chapter II of the Constitution, which is dedicated to the Parliament.	None
(See Articles 109 and 117 for Money Bills.)	None
Nevertheless, it remains within the judiciarys scope to ensure that the fundamental right under Article 21A of Part III is upheld.	None
In M.C. Mehta v. Union of India (vehicular pollution) (1998) 6 SCC 63 , this Court did not ignore the Article 21 right to life when deadly levels of pollution put the right at stake.	None
Nor will this Court ignore the Article 21A	None
right to education, when a dearth of quality schooling put it in jeopardy.	None
The Governments education programmes and expenditures, wanting in many respects, are an improvement over past performance.	None
They nearly fall short of the constitutional mark.	None
Lackluster performance in primary secondary schools is caused in part because Government places college students on a higher pedestal.	None
Money will not solve all our education woes, but a correction of priorities in step with the Constitutions mandate will go a long way.	None
Opposition to Compulsory Education Compulsory connotes enforcement.	None
The Parliament rejected an amendment that would have saved parents from penal penalties.	None
If education were not compulsory, who checks in with parents who have sent their children to work?	None
If no authorities inquire, the message is clear We, the State, do not care if your child goes to school.	None
Taking the opposing view, Shri G.M. Banatwalla wanted to make sure parents were not punished this word compulsion needs to be properly defined.	None
The word, compulsion is not to be related to the student or the parents.	None
Parents cannot be penalized for being too poor to send their children to school.	None
The word, compulsion has to be understood in relation to the State and the obligation of the State to provide for free education.	None
p. 523.	None
(See The Parliamentary Debates on Article 21A, p. 523, 28 November 2001 at Vol. 20, No. 610)	None
The Parliament had the opportunity to accept such a definition of compulsory.	None
But they chose otherwise.	None
Amendment number four, moved by Shri G.M. Banatwalla at p. 548, stated that Provided that in making any law to provide for free and compulsory education under this article, the State shall not  (b) enforce any penal sanctions on a parent or guardian.	None
Of paramount importance, this Amendment was negatived.	None
See p. 548.	None
Those who wanted a safehaven from penal sanction for parents lost.	None
From this vote, we know that the Parliament intended to allow for future legislation that would impose penal sanctions for violations of legislation under Article 21A. Conclusion on Free and Compulsory Education Given that so many children drop out of, or are absent from, school before they turn fourteen, free education alone cannot solve the problem.	None
The current patchwork of laws on compulsory education is insufficient.	None
Monetary fines do not go far enough to ensure that Article 21A is upheld.	None
A carrotandstick approach appears to be the best way to implement Article 21A. Financial incentive programmes have worked well in other countries.	None
We should follow their lead.	None
Once that is done, the Government should strictly enforce effective compulsory education laws.	None
Such a policy is bound to pay off.	None
In sum, the Central Government should enact legislation that (a) provides lowincome parents guardians with financial incentives such that they may afford to send their children to school (b) criminally penalizes those who receive financial incentives and despite such payment send their children to work (c) penalizes employers who preclude children from attending school or completing homework (d) the penalty should include imprisonment the aforementioned Bill would serve as an example.	None
The State is obligated under Article 21A to implement free and compulsory education in toto Until we have achieved the object of free and compulsory education, the Government should continue to increase the education budget (f) the Parliament should set a deadline by which time free and compulsory education will have reached every child.	None
This must be done within six months.	None
With regard to (a), the state cannot cite budgetary constraints or lack of resources as an excuse for failing to provide financial assistance incentives to poor parents.	None
See Hussainara Khatoon (supra), at page 107, para 10.	None
Article 21As reference to education must mean something.	None
This conclusion is bolstered by the Parliaments Statement of Objects and Reasons for Article 21A	None
The Constitution of India in a Directive Principle contained in article 45, has made a provision for free and compulsory education for all children up to the age of fourteen years within ten years of promulgation of the Constitution.	None
We could not achieve this goal even after 50 years of adoption of this provision.	None
The task of providing education to all children in this age group gained momentum after the National Policy of Education (NPE) was announced in 1986.	None
The Government of India, in partnership with the State Governments, has made strenuous efforts to fulfill this mandate and, though significant improvements were seen in various educational indicators, the ultimate goal of providing universal and quality education still remains unfulfilled.	None
In order to fulfill this goal, it is felt that an explicit provision should be made in the Part relating to Fundamental Rights of the Constitution.	None
With a view to making right to free and compulsory education a fundamental right, the Constitution (Eightythird Amendment)	None
Bill, 1997 was introduced in the Parliament to insert a new article, namely, article 21A conferring on all children in the age group of 6 to 14 years the right to free and compulsory education.	None
The said Bill was scrutinized by the Parliament Standing Committee on Human Resource Development and the subject was also dealt with in its 165th Report by the Law Commission of India.	None
After taking into consideration the report of the Law Commission of India and the recommendations of the Standing Committee of the Parliament, the proposed amendments in Part III, Part IV and Part IVA of the Constitution are being made which are as follows  The Bill seeks to achieve the above objects The Article seeks to usher in the ultimate goal of providing universal and quality education.	None
(emphasis supplied).	None
Implied within education is the idea that it will be quality in nature.	None
Current performance indicates that much improvement needs to be made before we qualify education with quality.	None
Of course, for children who are out school, even the best education would be irrelevant.	None
It goes without saying that all children aged six to fourteen must attend school and education must be quality in nature.	None
Only upon accomplishing both of these goals, can we say that we have achieved total compliance with Article 21A.	None
Though progress has been made, the Parliaments observation upon passing Art 21A still applies the goal of providing universal and quality education still remains unfulfilled.	None
Does the 93rd Amendment violate the Basic Structure of the Constitution by imposing reservation on unaided institutions?	None
Imposing reservation on unaided institutions violates the basic structure by obliterating citizens 19(1)(g) right to carry on an occupation.	None
Unaided entities, whether they are educational institutions or private corporations, cannot be regulated out of existence when they are providing a public service like education.	None
That is what reservation would do.	None
That is an unreasonable restriction.	None
When you do not take a single paisa of public money, you cannot be subjected to such restriction.	None
The 93rd Amendments reference to unaided institutions must be severed.	None
No unaided institution filed a writ petition in this case.	None
Had either this Court or respondents had an objection, they could have raised it at any time during the proceedings.	None
We listened to the parties for months.	None
We received voluminous written submissions from the parties, yet no objection was made with regard to the fact that no unaided institution had filed a writ petition.	None
While we would usually implead a party if we felt their presence was necessary to the resolution of the dispute, the facts of this case are peculiar.	None
The best lawyers in the country argued the case for both sides, and a brief from an unaided institution would not have added much if anything to the substance of the arguments.	None
The Government will likely target unaided institutions in the future.	None
At that time, this Court will have to go through this entire exercise de novo to determine if unaided institutions should be subject to reservation.	None
Such an exercise would unnecessarily cause further delay.	None
The fate of lakhs of students and thousands of institutions would remain up in the air.	None
(See Minerva Mills Ltd.	None
Others v. Union of India Others (1980) 3 SCC 625).	None
Therefore, looking to the extraordinary facts, I have decided to proceed with this aspect of the matter in the larger public interest.	None
Amendments by their very nature are often enabling provisions.	None
If they clear the way for future legislation that would in fact violate the basic structure, the Court need not wait for a potential violation to become an actual one.	None
It can strike the entire amendment ab initio.	None
The question of potential width was resolved in Minerva Mills (supra), paras 3839.	None
The Court acknowledged that it generally does not anticipate constitutional issues before they arise, but it held that circumstances required it to act before unconstitutional provisions could be passed under the authority of an unconstitutional amendment.	None
But, we find it difficult to uphold the preliminary objection because, the question raised by the petitioners as regards constitutionality of Sections 4 and 55 of the 42nd Amendment is not an academic or a hypothetical question.	None
The 42nd Amendment is there for anyone to see and by its Sections 4 and 55 amendments have been made to Articles 31C and 368 of the Constitution.	None
An order has been passed against the petitioners under Section 18A of the Industries (Development and Regulation) Act, 1951, by which the petitioners are aggrieved.	None
Besides there are two other relevant considerations which must be taken into account while dealing with the preliminary objection.	None
There is no constitutional or statutory inhibition against the decision of questions before they actually arise for consideration.	None
In view of the importance of the question raised and in view of the fact that the question has been raised in many a petition, it is expedient in the interest of justice to settle the true position.	None
Secondly, what we are dealing with is not an ordinary law which may or may not be passed so that it could be said that our jurisdiction is being invoked on the hypothetical consideration that a law may be passed in future which will injure the rights of the petitioners.	None
We are dealing with a constitutional amendment which has been brought into operation which, of its own force, permits the violation of certain freedoms through laws passed for certain purposes.	None
We, therefore, overrule the preliminary objection and proceed to determine the point raised by the petitioners.	None
emphasis added There is not one precise definition of the width test, however.	None
The test asks if an amendment is so wide that in effect (actual or potential), it goes beyond the Parliaments amending power.	None
Kesavananda, paras 531532	None
But that the real consequences can be taken into account while judging the width of the power is settled.	None
The Court cannot ignore the consequences to which a particular construction can lead To make such a determination, it follows that the Court should ask whether an amendment infringes constitutional limitations as opposed to those evolved from mere common law.	None
(See Nagaraj, para 103).	None
As a preliminary matter, I turn to the cases by which the basic structure doctrine has been established.	None
It has been stated that, Kesavananda had propounded the doctrine, the Indira Gandhi Election case had upheld it, and Minerva engraved it on stone.	None
(See Granville Austin, Working a Democratic Constitution, at page 506.	None
Kesavananda and its progeny provide that an amendment to the Constitution must not alter the Constitutions basic structure.	None
To reach a conclusion regarding a basic structure challenge, I employ the following general standard an amendment alters the basic structure if its actual or potential effect would be to damage a facet of the basic structure to such an extent that the facets original identity is compromised.	None
To determine if legislation infringes constitutional limitations and is thus invalid, we use the twostep effect test (also known as the impact or rights test).	None
Step One requires us to first ask if legislation affects a facet of the basic structure.	None
If it does, then at Step Two we ask if the effect on the facet of the structure is to such an extent that the facets original identity has been altered.	None
Applying the effect test is another way of saying that the form of an amendment is irrelevant it is the consequence thereof that matters.	None
(See Kesavanda at para 532 and I.R. Coelho v. State of Tamilnadu (2007) 2 SCC 1 at Conclusion (ii) at page 111).	None
The terms abridge and abrogate have been employed by this Court to distinguish between acceptable and unacceptable legislation.	None
Whether legislation abridges or abrogates is a question of degree.	None
Using these terms is another way of asking whether the legislation had such an effect that it changed the basic structure of the Constitution.	None
If legislation merely abridges the basic structure, the structures identity remains.	None
The legislation is upheld.	None
In this sense, the Parliament may take away or destroy fundamental rights by amending the Constitution, provided that the basic structure is not altered.	None
If it abrogates the basic structure, the structure and thus the Constitution lose their identities.	None
The legislation must be struck down.	None
This is determined on a casebycase basis by applying the effect test (impact rights tests).	None
(See Coehlo).	None
I further note that a total deprivation of fundamental rights, even in one limited area, may amount to an abrogation of the basic structure.	None
(See Minerva Mills, para 59).	None
Step One Does Article 15(5) affect a facet of the basic structure?	None
In the instant case, Article 15(5) expressly precludes the application of Article 19(1)(g).	None
Whenever reservations are implemented under Article 15(5), citizens are stripped of their fundamental rights under Article 19(1)(g).	None
By excluding Article 19(1)(g), Article 15(5) obviously affects Article 19(1)(g), a facet of the basic structure of the Constitution.	None
Step One is therefore cleared.	None
What is more, Article 19(1)(g) belongs to the Golden Triangle  Articles 14, 19 and 21 are the three fundamental rights that stand above the rest.	None
Writing for the majority in Minerva Mills, Justice Chandrachud provides an eloquent justification for shielding the Golden Triangle from attack.	None
To achieve a more egalitarian society, individual liberty must be protected Para 74 of Minerva Mills Three Articles of our Constitution, and only three, stand between the heaven of freedom into which Tagore wanted his country to awake and the abyss of unrestrained power.	None
They are Articles 14, 19 and 21.	None
Article 31C has removed two sides of that golden triangle which affords to the people of this country an assurance that the promise held forth by the Preamble will be performed by ushering an egalitarian era through the discipline of fundamental rights, that is, without emasculation of the rights to liberty and equality which alone can help preserve the dignity of the individual.	None
The Golden Triangles significance becomes clear when we consider that Government may suspend Article 14 and 19 rights in order to implement an emergency.	None
(See Articles 358 and 359) (prior to the 44th Amendment, all Part III rights could be curtailed during emergency this Amendment precludes the State from denying Articles 20 and 21 to citizens during emergency).	None
In a sense, democracy is only restored when the Triangle is returned to the citizens.	None
Without the Triangle, democracy is impossible para 63 Every State is goaloriented and claims to strive for securing the welfare of its people.	None
The distinction between the different forms of Government consists in that a real democracy will endeavour to achieve its objectives through the discipline of fundamental freedoms like those conferred by Articles 14 and 19.	None
Those are the most elementary freedoms without which a free democracy is impossible and which must therefore be preserved at all costs.	None
Besides, as observed by Brandies, J., the need to protect liberty is the greatest when Governments purposes are beneficent.	None
If the discipline of Article 14 is withdrawn and if immunity from the operation of that article is conferred, not only on laws passed by the Parliament but on laws passed by the State Legislatures also, the political pressures exercised by numerically large groups can tear the country asunder by leaving it to the legislature to pick and choose favoured areas and favourite classes for preferential treatment.	None
United States Supreme Court Justice Brandeis word of caution is relevant to todays dispute wherein the Government trumpets reservation in higher education as an answer to our ageold problems of poverty and caste.	None
At first blush, it sounds as if reservation in higher education would help the backward help themselves.	None
The road out of poverty is paved with education.	None
However, the devil is the details.	None
With elementary freedom on the line, I must carefully scrutinize those details.	None
The right to freedom under Article 19 has been long recognized as a natural and inalienable right that belongs to all citizens.	None
Indeed, what would Independence mean without it?	None
Chief Justice Sikri cites the following passage in Kesavananda at para 300 That article (Article 19) enumerates certain freedoms under the caption right to freedom and deals with those great and basic rights which are recognised and guaranteed as the natural rights inherent in the status of a citizen of a free country.	None
(Per Patanjali Sastri, C.J., in State of West Bengal v. Subodh Gopal Bose 1954 S.C.R. 587, 596).	None
With fundamental rights in jeopardy, I shall review the cases in which the basic structure doctrine has been implemented to invalidate constitutional amendments.	None
By looking at these cases synoptically, we get a sense as to how much damage the basic structure can withstand before crumbling.	None
In Kesavananda, the second part of Article 31C precluded courts from reviewing whether a law under Article 39(b) or (c) promoted the policy for which it was enacted.	None
This violated the basic structure.	None
Article 31C was introduced by the 25th Amendment.	None
In Indira Nehru Gandhi v. Raj Narain	None
Another (1975) Supp SCC 1, the Court struck Article 329A(4) as violative of the basic structure.	None
This provision appropriated the Courts power to adjudicate election laws, encroaching on the judiciary in violation of separation of powers.	None
See Justice Matthews opinion at para 325.	None
It was introduced by the 39th Amendment.	None
In Minerva Mills, the Court held sections 4 and 55 of the 42nd Amendment in violation of the basic structure.	None
Section 4 sought to expand 31C such that all laws giving effect to Directive Principles, not just those intended to promote Article 39(b) or (c), would be immune to an Article 14 or 19 challenge.	None
Section 55 would have barred judicial review of constitutional amendments.	None
In P. Sambamurthy v. State of A.P. (1987) 1 SCC 362 , the Court invalidated Article 371D(5), finding that the Parliament had violated the rule of law and consequently the basic structure, by removing judicial review from the High Court and placing it in the hands of one of the parties  the State Government.	None
In L. Chandra Kumar v. Union of India (1997) 3 SCC 261 , the Court held that Articles 323A2D and 323B3D violated the basic structure in that they removed judicial review of the High Courts and Supreme Court under Articles 226/227 and 32, respectively.	None
These articles were introduced by the 42nd Amendment to empower the Parliament or the State Legislatures to establish Tribunals for various substantive areas of law tax, labour, criminal, etc.	None
Two broad themes surface from these cases.	None
When judicial review is barred, democracy evaporates.	None
And when Fundamental Rights are at stake, they must be harmonized with, not made subject to, the Directive Principles.	None
Sections 4 and 55 of the 42nd Amendment were especially egregious violations of the basic structure.	None
Had Section 4 been upheld, citizens fundamental rights would have been at the mercy of one organ of Government.	None
If Governments always could be trusted, there would have been no need for Fundamental rights.	None
Mr. Palkhivala in oral arguments in Kesavananda, quoting from the learned Mr. H.M. Seervai, who was opposing counsel in that case.	None
Mr. Palkhivala was reading from Seervai, H.M., Fundamental Rights A Basic Issue, published in three installments in the Times of India, 14, 15, 16 February 1955.	None
(See Granville Austin at pages 263264 in Working a Democratic Constitution)	None
Government cannot be trusted that is precisely why we divide its powers into separate organs.	None
If it could be trusted, there would be no need for coequal branches in which power is shared.	None
Separation of powers is an axiom of democracy.	None
Had Section 55 of the 42nd Amendment been upheld, the basic structure of the Constitution could have been destroyed by a single slash.	None
Future constitutional amendments would not have been reviewed.	None
The impugned Amendment looks rather mild in comparison to the damage that would have been wrought by the 42nd Amendment.	None
The impugned legislation limits one fundamental right in one limited circumstance.	None
Yet an amendment need not be as invidious as the 42nd Amendment for us to invalidate it.	None
If the standard were that high, amendments could destroy the basic structure or the essence of the Constitution by a thousand slashes.	None
Since Kesavanandas time, many amendments have been passed and many challenges under the basic structure have been made.	None
This Court has used caution and has refrained from using the doctrine, even when it may have been justified.	None
For example, there were grounds for striking the entire 10th Schedule as violative of the basic structure in Kihoto Hollohan v Zachillhu Others 1992 Supp (2) SCC 651.	None
Rather than resort to the basic structure, this Court made a narrow ruling on procedural grounds.	None
(See S.P. Sathe, Judicial Activism in India Transgressing Borders and Enforcing Limits, 2nd Edn., 2002 (Oxford University Press) pages 9293).	None
The Court upheld the 10th Schedule, only severing a paragraph from the same.	None
I agree that an abundance of caution ought to be taken before employing the basic structure doctrine.	None
The violation must truly abrogate the basic structure.	None
Anything short of this standard must be upheld the will of the people, through their elected representatives, heard.	None
Before making such a determination, it is prudent to briefly revisit the rulings of two landmark cases P.A. Inamdar Others v. State of Maharashtra Others, (2005) 6 SCC 537 T.M.A. Pai Foundation Others v. State of Karnataka Others (2002) 8 SCC 481.	None
In Inamdar (supra), paras 2627 (sevenJudge Bench), unaided (minority and nonminority) professional institutions filed petitions to determine, inter alia, whether the State could impose quotas on unaided (minority and nonminority) institutions.	None
A sevenJudge Bench was constituted such that Islamic Academys clarification of Pai could be reviewed.	None
Islamic Academy was a fiveJudge Bench.	None
Given that Pai was an elevenJudge Bench, Inamdar could clarify but not overrule Pai.	None
At para 124, Inamdar held that the State cannot impose quotas on unaided (minority and nonminority) institutions.	None
To do so would nationalize seats, contrary to Pai.	None
(See Inamdar at para 125).	None
In dictum, Pai suggested that the State could compel unaided institutions to admit a reasonable percentage of students via reservation.	None
(Pai, para 68).	None
Inamdar clarified this point, stating that Pai should be read to mean that the State and unaided institutions may enter into consensual agreement regarding reservation.	None
(See Inamdar at para 126).	None
Unaided institutions (minority and nonminority) can admit as they choose, provided their process is fair, transparent, nonexploitative and meritbased.	None
Inamdar stated 124 So far as appropriation of quota by the State and enforcement of its reservation policy is concerned, we do not see much of difference between nonminority and minority unaided educational institutions.	None
We find great force in the submission made on behalf of the petitioners that the States have no power to insist on seat sharing in the unaided private professional educational institutions by fixing a quota of seats between the management and the State.	None
The State cannot insist on private educational institutions which receive no aid from the State to implement States policy on reservation for granting admission on lesser percentage of marks, i.e. on any criterion except merit.	None
As per our understanding, neither in the judgment of Pai Foundation nor in the Constitution Bench decision in Kerala Education Bill, which was approved by Pai Foundation, there is anything which would allow the State to regulate or control admissions in the unaided professional educational institutions so as to compel them to give up a share of the available seats to the candidates chosen by the State, as if it was filling the seats available to be filled up at its discretion in such private institutions.	None
This would amount to nationalization of seats which has been specifically disapproved in Pai Foundation.	None
Such imposition of quota of State seats or enforcing reservation policy of the State on available seats in unaided professional institutions are acts constituting serious encroachment on the right and autonomy of private professional educational institutions.	None
Such appropriation of seats can also not be held to be a regulatory measure in the interest of minority within the meaning of Article 30(1) or a reasonable restriction within the meaning of Article 19(6) of the Constitution.	None
Merely because the resources of the State in providing professional education are limited, private educational institutions, which intend to provide better professional education, cannot be forced by the State to make admissions available on the basis of reservation policy to less meritorious candidate.	None
Unaided institutions, as they are not deriving any aid from State funds, can have their own admissions if fair, transparent, nonexploitative and based on merit.	None
To the extent that Islamic Academy had approved of quotas in unaided institutions, a scheme in which the States could fix quota for seat sharing between management and the State, Islamic was overruled.	None
Inamdar at para 130 In T.M.A. Pai Foundation (supra) para 2 (eleven Judge Bench), private educational institutions, aided and unaided, filed writ petitions to challenge regulations that impeded their rights.	None
They wanted to establish and administer educational institutions, unfettered by Government interference.	None
para 2.	None
Reading Article 29(2) and 30(1) harmoniously, the sixJustice majority held that (1) unaided institutions could admit students free of Government interference, as long as their admission process was transparent and meritbased (2) minority aided institutions may still admit their own students, contingent upon admitting a reasonable number of nonminority students per the percentage provided by the State Government.	None
For our purposes, it is important to note that education falls within the meaning of occupation under 19(1)(g).	None
This is so because a large number of persons are employed as teachers and administrative staff.	None
For them, education is an occupation.	None
Pai stated 20 Article 19(1)(g) employs four expressions, viz., profession, occupation, trade and business.	None
Their fields may overlap, but each of them does have a content of its own.	None
Education is per se regarded as an activity that is charitable in nature	None
See The State of Bombay v. R.M.D. Chamarbaugwala, Education has so far not been regarded as a trade or business where profit is the motive.	None
Even if there is any doubt about whether education is a profession or not, it does appear that education will fall within the meaning of the expression occupation.	None
Article 19(1)(g) uses the four expressions so as to cover all activities of a citizen in respect of which income or profit is generated, and which can consequently be regulated under Article 19(6).	None
25	None
The establishment and running of an educational institution where a large number of persons are employed as teachers or administrative staff, and an activity is carried on that results in the imparting of knowledge to the students, must necessarily be regarded as an occupation, even if there is no element of profit generation.	None
It is difficult to comprehended that education, per se, will not fall under any of the four expressions in Article 19(1)(g).	None
Occupation would be an activity of a person undertaken as a means of livelihood or a mission in life.	None
emphasis added Stripping private unaided institutions of their right to select students would be unreasonable para 40	None
Any system of student selection would be unreasonable if it deprives the private unaided institution of the right of rational selection, which it devised for itself, subject to the minimum qualification that may be prescribed and to some system of computing the equivalence between different kinds of qualifications, like a common entrance test.	None
Such a system of selection can involve both written and oral tests for selection, based on principle of fairness.	None
Like Article 15(5) in the instant case, Unni Krishnan effectively nationalized education.	None
Pai overturned Unni Krishnan.	None
(See para 45).	None
38	None
The scheme in Unni Krishnans case has the effect of nationalizing education in respect of important features, viz., the right of a private unaided institution to give admission and to fix the fee.	None
By framing this scheme, which has led to the State Governments legislating in conformity with the scheme the private institutions are undistinguishable from the Government institutions curtailing all the essential features of the right of administration of a private unaided educational institution can neither be called fair or reasonable.	None
Pai traces the autonomy of institutions back to Chitralekha and Rajendran.	None
The proposition is simple he who funds or runs the institution holds the power to select students.	None
The State cannot ask these institutions to abridge this right in exchange for affiliation recognition.	None
The relevant paragraphs are reproduced hereunder 36 The private unaided educational institutions impart education, and that cannot be the reason to take away their choice in matters, inter alia, of selection of students and fixation of fees.	None
Affiliation and recognition has to be available to every institution that fulfills the conditions for grant of such affiliation and recognition.	None
The private institutions are right in submitting that it is not open to the Court to insist that statutory authorities should impose the terms of the scheme as a condition for grant of affiliation or recognition this completely destroys the institutional autonomy and the very objective of establishment of the institution.	None
In R. Chitralekha and Anr.	None
v. State of Mysore and Ors.citation omitted, while considering the validity of a vivavoce test for admission to a Government medical college, it was observed at page 380 that colleges run by the Government, having regard to financial commitments and other relevant considerations, would only admit a specific number of students.	None
It had devised a method for screening the applicants for admission.	None
While upholding the order so issued, it was observed that once it is conceded, and it is not disputed before us, that the State Government can run medical and engineering colleges, it cannot be denied the power to admit such qualified students as pass the reasonable tests laid down by it.	None
This is a power which every private owner of a College will have, and the Government which runs its own Colleges cannot be denied that power.	None
(italics added by Pai underscore is mine).	None
Again, in Minor P. Rajendran v. State of Madras and Ors., it was observed at page 795 that so far as admission is concerned, it has to be made by those who are in control of the Colleges, and in this case the Government, because the medical colleges are Government colleges affiliated to the University.	None
In these circumstances, the Government was entitled to frame rules for admission to medical colleges controlled by it subject to the rules of the university as to eligibility and qualifications.	None
The aforesaid observations clearly underscore the right of the colleges to frame rules for admission and to admit students.	None
The only requirement or control is that the rules for admission must be subject to the rules of the university as to eligibility and qualifications.	None
The Court did not say that the university could provide the manner in which the students were to be selected.	None
In the case of unaided private schools, maximum autonomy has to be with the management with regard to administration, including the right of appointment, disciplinary powers, admission of students and the fees to be charged.	None
Unaided institutions may admit students of their choice, subject to an objective and rational procedure of selection.	None
They might admit a small percentage of students belonging to the weaker sections of the society by granting those sections freeships or scholarships, if not granted by the Government.	None
See Pai at para 53.	None
Given a transparent and reasonable selection process, it is up to the institution to define merit according to its own values.	None
Pai stated The reputation of an educational institution is established by the quality of its faculty and students, and the educational and other facilities that the colleges has to offer.	None
The private educational institutions have a personality of their own, and in order to maintain their atmosphere and traditions, it is but necessary that they must have the right to choose and select the students who can be admitted to their courses of studies.	None
If is for this reason that in the St. Stephens College case, this Court upheld the scheme whereby a cutoff percentage was fixed for admission, after which the students were interviewed and thereafter selected.	None
While an educational institution cannot grant admission on its whims and fancies, and must follow some identifiable or reasonable methodology of admitting the students, any scheme, rule or regulation that does not give the institution the right to reject candidates who might otherwise be qualified according to say their performance in an entrance test, would be an unreasonable restriction under Article 19(6), though appropriate guidelines modalities can be prescribed for holding the entrance test a fair manner.	None
Even when students are required to be selected on the basis of merit, the ultimate decision to grant admission to the students who have otherwise qualified for the grant of admission must be left with the educational institution concerned.	None
However, when the institution rejects such students, such rejection must not be whimsical or for extraneous reasons.	None
The Court distinguishes between reasonable and unreasonable regulations by asking which functions lie at the heart of an institutions autonomy.	None
Regulations that strike at the core of autonomy are unreasonable.	None
For example, prescribing minimum qualifications for teachers is a reasonable regulation actually selecting the teachers is not.	None
But the essence of a private educational institution is the autonomy that the institution must have in its management and administration.	None
There, necessarily, has to be a difference in the administration of private unaided institutions and the Governmentaided institutions.	None
Whereas in the latter case, the Government will have greater say in the administration, including admissions and fixing of fees, in the case of private unaided institutions, maximum autonomy in the daytoday administration has to be with the private unaided institutions.	None
Bureaucratic or Governmental interference in the administration of such an institution will undermine its independence.	None
While an educational institution is not a business, in order to examine the degree of independence that can be given to a recognized educational institution, like any private entity that does not seek aid or assistance from the Government, and that exists by virtue of the funds generated by it, including its loans or borrowings, it is important to note that the essential ingredients of the management of the private institution include the recruiting students and staff, and the quantum of fee that is to be charged.	None
The same argument was framed in similar terms in St. Stephens College v. University of Delhi, 1992 (1) SCC 558.	None
In that case, the Court distinguished regulations based on whether they directly or indirectly affected management.	None
Those that indirectly affected management were reasonable those that directly affected the management of the institution were not.	None
Pai at para 125.	None
In St. Stephens, this Court referred to the earlier decisions, and with regard to Article 30(1) observed at page 596, paragraph 54, as follows But the standards of education are not a part of the management as such.	None
The standard concerns the body politic and is governed by considerations of the advancement of the country and its people.	None
Such regulations do not bear directly upon management although they may indirectly affect it.	None
The State, therefore has the right to regulate the standard of education and allied matters.	None
Once a private institution (nonminority) takes aid, it is subject to (1) reservation and (2) regulation of administration and maintenance of the institution.	None
Pai stated 71 While giving aid to professional institutions, it would be permissible for the authority giving aid to prescribe by rules or regulations, the conditions on the basis of which admission will be granted to different aided colleges by virtue of merit, coupled with the reservation policy of the state.	None
72	Arguments of Respondent
Once aid is granted to a private professional educational institution, the Government or the state agency, as a condition of the grant of aid, can put fetters on the freedom in the matter of administration and management of the institution.	None
The state, which gives aid to an educational institution, can impose such conditions as are necessary for the proper maintenance of the high standards of education as the financial burden is shared by the state.	None
I now query if the Parliament may subject Article 19(1)(g) to Article 15(5), when this Court has held that reservation in unaided institutions is an unreasonable restriction that cannot be saved by Article 19(6).	None
I answer this question in the affirmative.	None
The structure of our Constitution permits fundamental rights, and even the Golden Triangle of Articles 14, 19 and 21, to be abridged in limited circumstances.	None
To say that subjecting Articles 19(1)(g) to 15(5) violates the basic structure per se is to ignore the examples in which the most fundamental of rights is limited.	None
Article 16(4) expressly limits the right to formal equality in 16(1), a specific facet of Article 14.	None
In this light, Article 16(4) impliedly limits the general right to formal equality in Article 14.	None
The right to equality is expressed in the negative in 15(1) the State shall not discriminate based on religion, race, caste, etc.	None
In other words, the State shall treat citizens of different religions, races and castes equally.	None
Like Article 16(4), Article 15(4) limits 15(1)  another facet of Article 14 formal equality  such that egalitarian equality may be pursued.	None
Generally speaking, Articles 15(3) and (4) and 16(4) allow the State to impose affirmative action programs on the public sector.	None
Such provisions necessarily limit the right to formal equality.	None
If the right to equality, considered by some as a basic postulate of the Constitution, has been limited, a fortiori Article 19(1)(g) can be too.	None
Along these lines, I could turn to Articles 31A, 31B and 31C for further support.	None
Those Articles exclude challenges under Articles 14 and 19.	None
In agreement with Dr. Dhavans submission, I decline to rely on Articles 31A, 31B and 31C for support.	None
As explained in Minerva Mills, the Court had previously upheld Article 31A out of concern for stare decisis.	None
The Court never approved of the exclusion of Articles 14 and 19 on a principled basis.	None
Nor did it make a ruling as to whether the exclusion violated the basic structure.	None
(See para 7172 of Minerva Mills.	None
See also para 43 of Waman Rao, (1981) 2 SCC 362) .	None
A basic structure challenge becomes an issue of institutional competence.	None
Is it for the legislature to decide what is a reasonable restriction under 19(1)(g) read with 19(6)?	None
Or is it for the judiciary?	None
It is well established that the Parliament, expressing the will of the people, may enact amendments to overrule a judgment of this Court.	None
The First Parliament added Article 15(4) to the Constitution to overrule State of Madras v. Champakam Dorairajan, AIR 1951 SC 226 .	None
Other examples include the 77th Amendment, which overruled Sawhney I by adding Article 16(4A) the 81st Amendment further overruled Sawhney I by adding Art 16 (4B) the 82nd Amendment overruled S. Vinod Kumar Another v. Union of India Another (1996) 6 SCC 580 by amending Article 335 and the 85th Amendment overruled Virpal Singh Chauhann and Ajit Singh I by amending Article 16(4A), (1995) 6 SCC 684 and (1996) 2 SCC 715 , respectively.	None
Nevertheless, the duty to interpret the content of our fundamental rights has been left to the Courts.	None
The important point to be noted is that the content of a right is defined by the Courts.	None
The final word on the content of the right is of this Court.	None
(Nagaraj at para 21).	None
(emphasis added).	None
While the Parliament may amend the Constitution, it cannot alter the Constitutions basic structure.	None
(See Kesavananda, Indira Nehru Gandhi (Election Case), Minerva Mills, Sambamurthy, L. Chandra Kumar and Coelho).	None
Step Two Does Article 15(5) affect Article 19(1)(g) to such an extent that Article 19(1)(g)s original identity has been altered?	None
In other words, does Art 15(5) in effect merely abridge or completely abrogate Article 19(1)(g).	None
If the former, 15(5) stands.	None
If the latter, it falls.	None
As noted above, Coelho directs me to apply the impact rights test to determine whether the basic structure has been violated.	None
See Coehlo at Conclusion (ii) at page 111.	None
Thus, my query is whether to consider the impact on the entire constitutional framework, or to examine the effect on citizens engaged in unaided education as an occupation.	None
I think it is the latter.	None
I am not concerned here with those engaged in education in aided institutions.	None
One is naturally subject to greater regulation when one relies on Government funding.	None
(See Pai Inamdar).	None
Individual liberty and freedom, as protected by the Golden Triangle, must carry greater weight for those who set off on their own and refuse Government money.	None
This brings me to the question as to how large I should draw the circle when I ask who is affected by reservation in unaided institutions.	None
Justice Chandrachud provides that a total deprivation of fundamental rights, even in a limited area, can amount to abrogation of fundamental right just as a partial deprivation in every area can.	None
(See Minerva Mills, para 59).	None
Freedom under Article 19 belongs to individual citizens.	None
Article 19(1)(g) provides that all citizens shall have the right to practice any profession, or to carry on any occupation, trade or business.	None
The reference to all citizens means that each and every individual citizen possesses Article 19 rights.	None
For the impugned legislation to fall, it need not touch every sphere of society.	None
If even one individuals freedom has been curtailed, this Court is duty bound to entertain his or her claim.	None
It is he or she who possesses the Article 19(1)(g) right to carry on an occupation.	None
If 15(5) were implemented, the educator in unaided institutions would still have students to educate.	None
I use educator in the broadest sense of the term and include teachers, professors, lecturers, faculty, staff, administrators and those who finance institutions.	None
Without one of the aforementioned, the institution cannot function properly.	None
Though affected by reservation, the educator still has a job.	None
His occupation remains intact.	None
Students will come.	None
Classes will commence.	None
Marks will be distributed.	None
The greatest impact on the educator is that neither he nor his institution will choose whom to teach.	None
Almost half of the time (49.5), the State would decide for them.	None
Selecting students or employees goes to the heart of an organizations autonomy.	None
The essence of an unaided educational institution is the freedom to manage its affairs, according to Pai at paragraph 55.	None
That is, the essential ingredients of the management of the private institution include the recruiting of students and staff.	None
The same argument was framed in similar terms (at para 54) in St. Stephens College (regulations imposing standards of education upheld, because they do not bear directly upon management although they may indirectly affect it).	None
This Court has stated in Pai as clarified by Inamdar that subjecting unaided institutions is an unreasonable restriction.	None
As noted, Article 19(6) provides no safe haven for reservations.	None
The Governmentimposed selection of students in turn has wideranging consequences for unaided institutions and their educators.	None
I am required to examine the effect of the impugned Amendment.	None
At least four problems will likely arise (1) academic standards suffer (2) attracting and retaining good faculty becomes more difficult (3) the incentive to establish a first rate unaided institution is diminished (4) and ultimately the global reputation of our unaided institutions is severely compromised.	None
First, once the State tells them whom to teach, standards of excellence will suffer.	None
This is because those institutions will no longer be able to admit the highestscoring students.	None
As good as some of our institutions are, they do not teach blank slates.	None
The best universities are the best, in part, because they attract the best students.	None
The same can be said for almost any organization.	None
In the case of higher education, the universities that admit the best will likely churn out the best.	None
The precise extent to which the university made the best so good cannot be qualified.	None
The point is that universities alone cannot produce qualified job candidates.	None
Forced to admit students with lower marks, the universitys final product will not be as strong.	None
Once the creamy is excluded, cutoff marks would likely drop considerably in order to fill the 27 quota for non creamy layer OBCs.	None
When the creamy layer is not removed, as in the case of Tamil Nadu, the difference in cut off marks for the general and backward categories may be insignificant.	None
(See para 408 of Sawhney I).	None
Of course, the extent to which standards of excellence would suffer would vary by institution.	None
As I mention below, I urge the Government to set OBC cut off marks no lower than 10 marks below that of the general category.	None
This is only a recommendation, however.	None
It may never be adopted.	None
Second, reservations weaken the incentive to establish unaided institutions if the State usurps the right to select students, would one still spend the time and money to establish an unaided institution?	None
The question is all the more relevant today.	None
Counsel for petitioners posit that tomorrows knowledge economy requires a welleducated populace.	None
Welleducated does not imply a string of degrees from less than taxing institutions.	None
Rather, it means that one will possess the skills, knowledge and creativity to compete globally.	None
Our unaided institutions must remain places where these traits are refined.	None
Third, those inclined to teach the brightest students have even less of a reason to leave private sector jobs for the teaching profession or to join the profession in the first place.	None
Brightest would come with an asterisk.	None
They would be the brightest available under the Governments reservation scheme.	None
These potential teachers may ask themselves how will I teach a class in which half the students are advanced relative to the other half?	None
In many institutions, the shortage of toprate faculty will only get worse.	None
Fourth, reservations may have a negative impact on students seeking employment in the burgeoning knowledge economy.	None
Recruiters have begun to trickle into campuses.	None
They hail from domestic as well as international entities, and they too may take note of reservations in unaided institutions.	None
The effect on educators, from the top down, would be felt.	None
For them, little more than a semblance of occupation would remain.	None
Given the dramatic effect that reservations would have on educators, the unaided institutions in which they teach and, consequently, society as a whole, Article 19(1)(g) has been more than abridged.	None
When education is effectively nationalized, freedom stands obliterated.	None
The identity of the Constitution is altered when unreasonable restrictions make a fundamental right meaningless.	None
The 93rd Amendments imposition of reservation on unaided institutions has abrogated Article 19(1)(g), a basic feature of the Constitution, in violation of our Constitutions basic structure.	None
Therefore, I sever the 93rd Amendments reference to unaided institutions as ultra vires of the Constitution.	None
The case law on severability asks the following question had the Parliament known its provision would be severed would it still have passed the rest of the legislation?	None
(See R.M.D. Chamarbaugwalla (supra)).	None
At page 943 of R.M.D. Chamarbaugwalla (supra), the Court relied in part on The State of Bombay Another v. F.N. Balsara (1951)	None
SCR 682 , where the question at issue was whether the Bombay Prohibition Act was valid Sections 12 and 13 of the Act imposed restrictions on the possession, consumption and sale of liquor, which had been defined in s. 2(24) of the Act as including (a) spirits of wine, methylated spirits, wine, beer, toddy and all liquids consisting of or containing alcohol, and (b) any other intoxicating substance which the Provincial Government may, by notification in the Official Gazette, declare to be liquor for the purposes of this Act.	None
Certain medicinal and toilet preparations had been declared liquor by notification issued by the Government under s. 2(24)(b).	None
The Act was attacked in its entirety as violative of the rights protected by Art. 19(1)(f).	None
But this Court held that the impugned provisions were unreasonable and therefore void in so far as medicinal and toilet preparations were concerned, but valid as to the rest.	None
Then, the contention was raised that as the law purports to authorise the imposition of a restriction on a fundamental right in language wide enough to cover restrictions both within and without the limits of constitutionally permissible legislative action affecting such right, it is not possible to uphold it even so far as it may be applied within the constitutional limits, as it is not severable.	None
In rejecting this contention, the Court observed These items being thus treated separately by the legislature itself and being severable, and it is not being contended, in view of the directive principles of State policy regarding prohibition, that the restrictions imposed upon the right to possess or sell or buy or consume or use those categories of properties are unreasonable, the impugned sections must be held valid so far as these categories are concerned.	None
This decision is clear authority that the principle of severability is applicable even when Acts invalidity arises by reason of its contravention of constitutional limitations.	None
At page 944, the court in R.M.D. Chamarbaugwalla sought guidance from American case law on severability In discussing the effect of a severability clause, Brandies, J. observed in Dorchy v. State of Kansas (1924) 264 US 286 that it provides a rule of construction, which may sometimes aid in determining that intent.	None
But it is an aid merely not an inexorable command.	None
The weight to be attached to a classification of subjects made in the statute itself cannot, in our opinion, be greater than that of a severability clause.	None
The court in R.M.D Chambarbaugwalla went on to cite Patanjali Sastri, C.J., in The State of Bombay Another v. The	None
United Motors (India) Ltd.	None
Others (1953)	None
SCR 1069 dealing with the contention that a law authorizing the imposition of a tax on sales must be declared to be wholly void because it was bad in part as transgressing constitutional limits observed It is a sound rule to extend severability to include separability in enforcement in such cases, and we are of opinion that the principle should be applied in dealing with taxing statutes in this country.	None
Here, I believe the Parliament would have gone forward without unaided institutions.	None
While some Members of Parliament sought to overrule Pai and Inamdar, the Parliaments actions speak louder than its words.	None
Once it had passed Article 15(5), it limited itself to imposing greater reservations on aided institutions.	None
Had unaided institutions been the Parliaments priority, it could have included them in the Reservation Act.	None
It seems that the Parliaments intent is to pass as much reservation as possible.	None
That would explain why it has gone forward with 27 reservation for OBCs without confirming that at least 27 of the population is OBC.	None
For these reasons, I conclude that had the Parliament known that unaided institutions were going to be severed, it would have nevertheless carried out its reservation scheme for aided institutions.	None
The Casteless and Classless Society versus Castebased Reservation	None
The caste system is peculiar to this country.	None
Perhaps the entire society has been divided on the basis of caste.	None
This social problem can be compared to some extent with that of American society.	None
In the U.S., the problem of racial discrimination has existed for centuries.	None
The cases of affirmative action decided in the United States are relevant.	None
They show us how that society has dealt with the problem of racial discrimination.	None
At the outset, I would like to make it clear that decisions of foreign countries are not binding on Indian courts.	None
Indian Courts have not adopted American standards of review.	None
But the judgments delivered by U.S. courts on affirmative action have great persuasive value and they may provide broad guidelines as to how we should tackle our prevailing condition.	None
A large number of English laws have been inherited by India and America.	None
English and American cases are frequently cited by our courts.	None
We need to keep our window open and permit the light of knowledge to enter from any source.	None
In this light, I shall refer to some US decisions.	None
Affirmative Action cases and standards of review from the United States In 1978	None
, Regents of the University of California v. Bakke put an end to reservation (quotas) in education (reserving 16 out of 100 seats for minorities in medical school deemed unconstitutional).	None
(438 U.S. 265).	None
Justice Powells concurring judgment is considered the key opinion in the case.	None
Justice Powell concluded that diversity was a compelling State interest that could withstand strict scrutiny.	None
Relying on Bakke, the court later reaffirmed preferential treatment in college admissions as a means to ensure diversity in the classroom  racial diversity being just one among many types of diversity (overcoming personal adversity and family hardship was another form of diversity), (See Grutter v. Bollinger, 539 U. S. 306, 338 (2003)).	None
The Grutter Case insisted that universities make an individualized evaluation of a student seeking admission, rather than one that mechanically accepted or rejected students on the basis of race.	None
(Grutter at 337).	None
Such an evaluation would ensure that race was only considered as one type of diversity, rather than a pretext for achieving racial balance.	None
Quotas could not be covertly installed in the name of diversity.	None
This reasoning led the court to strike down an admission scheme that automatically assigned more points to minority students than to residents of the State or to athletes, for example.	None
(Gratz v. Bollinger, 539 U.S. 244, 270).	None
Justice OConner for the majority in Grutter came to a very significant conclusion.	None
She suggested that there was time limit on preferential treatment for certain races as a means of promoting diversity.	None
Justice OConnor stated we expect that 25 years from now, the use of racial preferences will no longer be necessary to further the interest approved today.	None
In Parents Involved in Community Schools v. Seattle School District No.1 et al, reported in 168 Lawyers Ed.	None
2d 508 517 (2007), school districts used a students race to assign that student to a particular school within the district.	None
In Seattle, this was done to achieve racial balance amongst the districts schools.	None
One school should not be overwhelmingly white, another all nonwhite.	None
Unlike the system approved in Grutter, race was not just one among many types of diversity that was considered by the district in assigning students.	None
Seattle at 525.	None
Instead, it was, at times, the decisive factor.	None
The court held the programmes unconstitutional.	None
Chief Justice Roberts summed up the pluralitys view on racial classifications the way to stop discrimination on the basis of race is to stop discriminating on the basis of race.	None
This was far from a complete victory for the plurality.	None
In his concurring opinion, Justice Kennedy found the programmes unconstitutional.	None
However, he would not go so far as to treat all racial balancing as per se unconstitutional.	None
He considered the plurality opinion to represent an alltoounyielding insistence that race cannot be a factor in instances, when, in his view, it may be taken into account.	None
(Seattle at 565).	None
Justice Kennedy found that schools have a compelling interest to prevent racial isolation or achieve a diverse student population.	None
(Seattle at 572).	None
Like Justice Powells concurring opinion in Bakke, Justice Kennedys concurring opinion leaves the door open for further use of racial classification for socalled benign purposes in school admissions.	None
More important than any one case are the standards by which the court scrutinized discriminatory legislation.	None
Of course, Indian courts have not accepted the principles of narrow tailoring and strict scrutiny.	None
Nevertheless, we should seek guidance from any corner and permit the light from any quarter.	None
Whenever legislation is challenged as unconstitutional, courts must ask themselves how much deference they will give to the legislature.	None
The answer is that it depends on the nature of the impugned legislation.	None
The United States Supreme Court has evolved three standards of review for Government action that treats different people differently.	None
The first is the rational basis standard.	None
When the classification is rationally related to any legitimate Government purpose, the court defers to the State and upholds the classification.	None
This is the most deferential of the three standards.	None
The second standard is intermediate scrutiny, which is less deferential to Government.	None
Here, the court asks whether the classification is substantially related to any important Government purpose.	None
The third and highest level of review is known as strict scrutiny, whereby the court requires that the classification are narrowly tailored to a compelling state interest.	None
Strict scrutiny test is the least deferential to Government.	None
Of the classifications on which there is case law, the one that most closely resembles caste is race.	None
This is because both are immutable traits.	None
They are used by the powerful, or those seeking power, to justify oppression.	None
Racism and casteism have long haunted both Nations.	None
In the United States, race raises red flags.	None
It is often, though not always, reviewed under strict scrutiny Government action dividing people by race is inherently suspect because such classifications promote notions of racial inferiority and lead to a politics of racial hostility, (Croson at 102 L. Ed. 2d 854) and racial classifications are simply too pernicious to permit any but the most exact connection between the justification and the classification.	None
(Gratz v. Bollinger, 539 U.S. 244, 270 (quoting J. Stevens dissent in Fullilove v. Klutznick, 448 U.S. 448, 537)).	None
Legislation whose text does not classify based on race is considered facially neutral.	None
When facially neutral legislation has a disproportionate impact on a particular race, American courts ask whether it was passed with an intention to discriminate.	None
If no intention is found, the rational basis test applies.	None
See Hernandez v New York, 500 U.S. 352 (1991) (quoting from Arlington Heights v. Metropolitan Housing Development Corp., 429 U.S. 252, 264265 (1977)	None
A court addressing this issue must keep in mind the fundamental principle that official action will not be held unconstitutional solely because it results in a racially disproportionate impact. . . .	None
Proof of racially discriminatory intent or purpose is required to show a violation of the Equal Protection Clause.	None
See also Washington v. Davis, 426 U.S. 229, 239 (1976).	None
The exception to this rule is Yick Wo v. Hopkins, 118 U.S. 356 (1886), where extreme disproportionate impact warranted greater scrutiny.	None
Where there is disproportionate impact and discriminatory intention, then even facially neutral legislation triggers strict scrutiny.	None
However, in this framework, affirmative action classifies on the face of legislation and automatically gets strict scrutiny treatment.	None
As I have observed, American courts carefully review racial classifications.	None
Given that the 93rd Amendment on its face discriminates against general category students, we should give it careful scrutiny.	None
The Article 14 right to formal equality deserves as much.	None
If 49.5 castebased reservation was upheld in Sawhney I for Government employment, it follows that 49.5 castebased reservation is permitted in aided educational institutions.	None
While I compelled by Sawhney I to hold that the impugned legislation passes careful scrutiny with respect to reservation in aided institutions, its implementation is contingent upon the directions given in this opinion.	None
The Framers ultimate goal the Classless and Casteless society Did the original Framers intend to provide castebased reservation in education to the lower classes?	None
No, the original Framers did not.	None
Soon after the Constitution was adopted, the very same Framers acted quickly to permit reservation for SC ST SEBCs in education by adding Art 15(4), vide the First Amendment, to the Constitution.	None
In doing so, they deviated from their own goal the casteless society would have to wait.	None
In Sawhney I, the Court upheld this decision and bound us to a certain degree on this point.	None
I have no choice but to uphold the impugned legislation by which the Government may still identify SEBCs, in part, by using caste.	None
Castebased reservation was initially a temporary measure that was to only last for ten years.	None
The original Framers considered castebased reservation a necessary evil.	None
Thus, they limited it in time.	None
Extending this time limit has only exacerbated casteism.	None
The Parliamentary Debates clearly reflect that the ultimate aim of reservation was a casteless and classless society for India.	None
To this end, reservation should only be given for a specific period of time.	None
If these reservations or benefits have to continue perpetually, then the basic goal of achieving casteless and classless society would never be accomplished.	None
The need for castebased reservation has worn out over time.	None
Evidence for the proposition that caste is no longer a valid determinant of ones ability to move up in society is strong.	None
More than the way society judges you based on caste, the relevant question is whether caste precludes you from rising.	None
If caste doesnt, then what does?	None
The answer is simple money.	None
Income is a much better determinant of educational achievement than caste.	None
The table below was derived from the Reproductive Child and Health Survey, 20022004 (600,000 households surveyed).	None
Average years of schooling SC  OBC  Upper caste Hindu Poorest Rural Quintile 1.6  1.7  2.2 Richest Rural Quintile 5.1  5.5  6.1 For the upper caste, caste barely helps.	None
These numbers indicate that it is ones income, not caste, that makes a real difference in determining how much schooling one completes.	None
Therefore, if income be the bar to education, economic criteria should be the means by which we identify beneficiaries of special provisions under Article 15(5).	None
No original intent to provide castebased quotas in education As drafters, the original Framers were prolific.	None
They made our Constitution the worlds longest  removing as many doubts as possible and in that way limiting the Courts role.	None
The Constitution contains a number of Articles that reserve seats for various groups.	None
The original Framers, however, imposed various limitations on reservation.	None
These limitations provide insight into the original Framers compromise between formal and substantial egalitarian equality.	None
Reservation is only provided for certain groups (SC, ST and backward classes) in certain areas of the public sector.	None
(See Article 16(4) (reservation of posts in Government service for backward classes), Article 330 (reservation of seats for SC and ST in the Lok Sabha) and Article 332 (reservation of seats for SC and ST in Legislative Assemblies of the States)).	None
Dr Ambedkar stated that the report of the Minorities Committee provided that all minorities should have two benefits or privileges, namely representation in the legislatures and representation in the services.	None
(emphasis added) (See CAD, 26 August 1949, vol. 9, p. 702).	None
Given this limitation, we must take extra caution when reviewing the constitutionality of adding additional benefits.	None
Article 334 fixed a 10year time limit on the legislative reservations provided in Articles 330 and 332.	None
In the discussion regarding draft Article 292, Sardar Hukam Singh said, we are accepting this reservation of seats in legislative bodies as an unavoidable evil for the present, thought it is only for the Scheduled Castes and scheduled tribes.	None
(See p. 645, Constituent Assembly Debates, Vol. 9, 24 August 1949).	None
Shri Singhs comment sums up the limitations on legislative reservation.	None
OBC SEBCs were excluded, and reservations were limited in time.	None
Unlike the legislative reservations, Article 16(4) contains no fixed time limit.	None
It does, however, preclude the State from making reservations in Government service if the backward classes are adequately represented.	None
The idea is that, at some point in time, the backward classes would no longer need reservations.	None
In discussing draft Article 10 (Article 16(4) of the Constitution), Pandit Hirday Nath Kunzru stated We are all aware that when the Report of the Minorities Committee was considered by the House, the entire House was anxious that reservations of whatever kind should be done away with as quickly as possible.	None
Whatever protection might be considered necessary now, should be granted temporarily only, so that the population of the county might become fully integrated, and no community or class might be tempted to claim special advantage for itself.	None
(CAD Vol.7 dated 30th November 1948, p. 681) (emphasis supplied) Instead of moving to remove reservations, the Parliament has gone the other way by extending time limits and adding beneficiaries.	None
Article 15(5) is just the latest example.	None
While the original Framers went out of their way to put SC ST in the Parliament and State Assemblies and SC ST backward classes in Government service, they did not reserve a single classroom seat.	None
Instead, Article 29(2) prohibited castebased discrimination in admissions, and Article 15(2) prohibited castebased discrimination in general.	None
Education was to remain reservationfree.	None
When preferential treatment was given in regard to education, it was limited to educational grants.	None
There was no question of doling out reservations for special groups.	None
Article 337 provided educational grants to AngloIndian schools for the benefit of that community.	None
In the spirit of conciliation, the original Framers allowed the grants that were already going to those schools to continue for 10 years.	None
(See p 936941 of Constituent Assembly Debates, Vol. 8 1949).	None
Rather than advocate for reservation, the original Framers preferred free compulsory education and scholarships.	None
In the debate on Draft Article 294, Shri Brajeshwar Prasad stated that reservation in legislative bodies would fail to uplift SC ST.	None
Instead, he suggested that it should be laid down clearly in express terms that  free education shall be imparted to them.  	None
and for the tribals and Harijans provision must be made in the constitution that free agricultural lands should be given to them.	None
If we cannot give any one of these, I am quite clear in my own mind that by giving them a few seats here and there, their economic condition and their educational level will in no way be improved.	None
(CAD, Vol. 9, 24 August 1948, pages 663664) (emphasis supplied)	None
Shri Prasads comments are relevant because he recognizes the limited effect of reservation.	None
Rather than reserve seats for a few, he advocated for free education for all.	None
In the debate regarding Article 15 of the Constitution, Syed Abdur Rouf summed up the essence of the provision The intention of this article is to prohibit discrimination against citizens.	None
(See p. 650 of CAD, Vol.7, 29 Nov 1948).	None
This intention was only qualified for women and children.	None
In fact, the original Framers rejected an amendment that would have watered down Article 15s prohibition against discrimination.	None
Prof. K. T. Shah sought special protection for SC ST.	None
He wanted to ensure that Article 15 would allow SC ST to benefit from affirmative action.	None
To this end, he introduced an amendment that would have altered 15(3) to read as follows Nothing in this article shall prevent the State from making any special provision for women and children or for the Scheduled Castes or backward tribes, for their advantage, safeguard or betterment.	None
(Shah amendment in italics).	None
Prof. Shah proposed the amendment so that any special discrimination in favour of them may not be regarded as violating the basic principles of equality for all classes of citizens in the country.	None
They need and must be given for some time to come at any rate, special treatment in regard to education, in regard to opportunity for employment, and in many other cases where their present inequality, the present backwardness is only a hindrance to the rapid development of the country.	None
equality is not to be equality of name only or on paper only, but equality of fact.	None
pages 655656 CAD, Vol. 7, 29 November 1948.	None
(emphasis supplied)	None
